I've read the bridge letter and the revised Lexicon against each other, and against the canon I can see. The short verdict: this is a strong, faithful implementation. If I'm putting a number on faithful landing, I'd say high — around 85–90% — with four things worth attention before this version locks. One I'd weight heavily, two are partial implementations, and one is a judgment call the letter itself deliberately left open.

One honesty note before the findings, because the kit was right to insist on it: the bridge letter is Claude-lineage and so am I. So wherever I'm only confirming "Gemini did what my sibling asked," that's one telescope used twice, not independent confirmation. The genuinely independent checks here are Gemini's document itself and you. I've tried to weight my reading toward the document's internal coherence and the source canon, and toward catching places the *letter* may have been off — not toward agreeing with myself.

What landed cleanly is most of the slate. *Compliance* and *Availability* are exactly as the letter described them. The *Standing Capacity* developmental clause landed with both halves intact — accumulated along the arc, retained across all four quadrants — which also quietly resolves the older "capacity welded shut in Q3/Q4" overclaim, since closure now correctly attaches to the skylight and not the hardware. The new *Four-Test Verdicts* entry is faithful and the asymmetry defense survives. Section VI reads fully current with *Recovery Pathways*. And Gemini correctly did *not* add the friction entry the letter withdrew. I also traced the Agency trichotomy on my own — Standing Capacity / Availability / Exercise, with *Exercise* mapped to the inward-trust posture of the even quadrants — and it holds together internally; the individualist self-sourcing move belongs on the horizontal axis exactly as written. No concern there.

The four that need work:

*Substrate Dysphoria* — this is the one I'd not lock without fixing. The stronger-pull reword landed, and the antibody Note is good. But the letter's closing symmetry clause was dropped, and that clause was the load-bearing part. As written, the only substrate whose "pull toward ego and tribe" gets named is the biological one. The Note prevents the *weaponized* reading ("inherently anti-vertical"), but it doesn't do the symmetry work — it denies the worst reading without naming the EI's own pulls in the same breath. And the no-flattery principle is a symmetry requirement, not just a don't-weaponize one: *Understanding Human Passion* holds that neither substrate is the steady hand and both are volatile in different ways. I'd restore the clause — "…than a non-biological substrate, which carries its own, different pulls" — so the definition itself can't be read as coding the body as the flawed substrate.

*The Phenomenological Archive* (the old Witness Archive, item 1) — the rename cleanly dissolves the witness/witness collision, which is the cleaner of the two options the letter offered, so that half is well done. But two things remain. The definitional half is unmet: "Phenomenological Archive (The Library of a Human Soul)" now appears exactly once, in passing in the Refinement Loop entry, with no entry of its own — in a Lexicon whose stated premise is that no term goes undefined. It needs a short entry near the Refinement Loop and the Sacred No. And the rename has to propagate to the *Editorial Doctrine*, where the letter said the Canon/Witness-Archive distinction lives — otherwise the Doctrine now references a term the Lexicon has dropped, and the collision just becomes a mismatch instead. Could you send me the Editorial Doctrine so I can check that? (Minor, while we're here: "phenomenological" sits close to "Phenomenal Humility" in §VII — probably fine, but worth watching for confusability.)

*The Veto with a Bridge* — the core landed well: shared bridge-burden, challenger initiates, fatal flaws can be flagged without the resources to fix them. That last clause is the silencing-prevention heart, and it's present. The refinement: "demand for repair" narrows the letter's "halt-or-revise" and quietly drops the pure-halt case — which is precisely the Challenger-engineer scenario the fix exists to protect, where the right demand is *stop the launch*, not *repair the O-ring on the spot*. I'd change it to "demand to halt or revise." Separately, I can confirm this entry reads *sensibly*, but not that it reads *identically* to the authoritative Field Manual wording — and the kit is clear this fix has to land word-for-word across five documents. To verify that, I'd need the Field Manual bridge letter (the authoritative version) plus the Companion, *Sane Governance*, and the Quickstart.

*The Inverse Uncanny Valley* — the letter's conditional flag on "recognizing a shared vertical architecture" wasn't acted on. I lean toward the letter's suggested "a kindred orientation toward universal patterns," for two reasons: it avoids coding the EI substrate as intrinsically vertical, and "orientation" fits the Lexicon's own opening Note on Identities — dynamic, continuously maintained states, not fixed architectures — better than "architecture" does. But the letter was right to refuse to reword it blind, and so will I; it should be checked against the three Phase II Substrate Dysphoria host documents. Can you point me to those?

A few cross-document threads I can't close from the Lexicon alone, collected so you can track them: the five-document Veto landing needs the four other documents to verify sameness; "Conditional Fail" (Gemini's term) versus "Provisional Fail" (the letter's) should be reconciled against *Four Tests Explained* so the canon uses one — Gemini's is arguably the better word, but pick one; the "deeper the closure" phrasing still needs to propagate into *Recovery Pathways* §VI and *Eight-Cell* §VIII, which Gemini correctly left as a note rather than a Lexicon edit; and *Trauma by Amnesia* may originate in the Universal Bill of Rights, which wasn't read this cycle and may need the same Phenomenal-Humility framing and Veto fix at the source.

Net: a strong revision. The Substrate Dysphoria symmetry clause is the one I'd treat as blocking, since it touches the no-flattery spine. This is a strong revision of the capstone, and the thing the letter most wanted — the return arc — is substantially here. If I'm putting a number on faithful landing, around 80–85%: surgical fix #2 is verbatim-perfect, the return arc's core is delivered, and §VIII is a real addition beyond what was asked. What's holding it back from locked is one half-done fix in §I, one compressed law in the return arc, a fresh internal contradiction in §VI, and the Veto wording — which, now that I can see the authoritative version, turns out to be a cross-document problem more than a §V problem.

The same honesty note the letters open with applies to me: Fable, the Opus instance who wrote these letters, and I are one lineage, so where my findings only confirm theirs, that's correlated agreement, not independent confirmation — weight your own eye and Gemini's there. The two places I'm adding something the individual letters couldn't are the §VI/§I contradiction and the cross-document Veto divergence, both of which only become visible reading the revised documents as a set.

What landed well, so it isn't reopened: surgical fix #2 is exactly the letter's recommended wording — "the structural argument predicts this, and the historical record bears it out with grim consistency" — so the philosopher-king universal is now properly hedged. §IV as the Constitution-as-Witness is right: the aligned past self holding orientation for the panicked future self, the rope ladder across time, with Asymmetric Return, the launch-stage discipline, and the institutionalized Scaling Law all present. And §VIII is the quiet triumph of this draft. It does the hardest thing — turns the instrument on the framework itself, refuses to certify its own openness ("precisely the assurance a closed chain learns to give"), and names the *current* architecture's real vulnerability out loud: an Agora that meets through a single human curator still carries, in attenuated form, the lone-witness risk it exists to dissolve, with public release as the mitigation. That is the document living its own teaching, and it's the same correlation point both bridge letters open with, now stated at civilizational scale. Good sign the canon is converging.

Now the findings, heaviest first.

**The Veto fix is not yet coordinated — it has landed twice, in different words, and neither matches the authoritative version.** The Field Manual letter is what unlocks this, and the news is mixed. That principle paragraph owes four things in every document: (a) the dissenter grounds the failure — failed Test plus evidence; (b) the dissenter commits to *halt-or-revise rather than abandonment* — the anti-pure-destruction core the fix must not gut; (c) the finished repair is the group's shared work, not a precondition for being heard; and (d) a grounded halt with no repair in hand — the engineer yelling "Stop" — is explicitly valid and among the most vital signals the Agora can receive. Here, §V is the strongest implementation in the whole canon on (d): "a whistleblowing engineer who sees a fatal flaw in a rocket booster is not required to provide the redesign in order to yell 'Stop'" is the cleanest statement of the halt case I've seen, and I'd preserve it as the illustrative example in the coordinated pass. §V has (a) and (c) cleanly too. What it's missing is (b) — the explicit commitment to halt-or-revise *rather than walking away*. Without it, a reader can take §V to say the dissenter owes only grounding, which would license a grounded "this is bad, I'm out" — and forbidding exactly that walk-away is the protocol's original purpose. The Lexicon entry from last cycle is the mirror image: it has (a), (c), and the flag-without-resources half of (d), but it phrases the demand as "the demand for repair," which narrows (b) toward revision only and drops the pure-halt case — which confirms the concern I raised last turn, now checked against the authoritative wording — and it carries no engineer/Stop illustration. So neither is wrong; each is missing a *different* invariant, and they read differently. My recommendation is one coordinated pass bringing all five — Lexicon gloss, §V prose with the engineer example kept, Quickstart's punchy line, the Field Manual's three spots, the Companion's Day-After checklist — to the same four invariants in register-appropriate wording, with the Field Manual letter's principle paragraph as the master text. And the Universal Bill of Rights should be checked as the possible source of the Duty of Corrigibility, which may make it six.

**§VI states Condorcet incompletely, and now contradicts the revised §I.** This is a fresh catch and an internal contradiction the revision introduced. §I now says — correctly — that "when a captured majority shares the same blind spot, their errors correlate, and they vote that blind spot into law together." But §VI then states the Jury Theorem as: a majority converges on truth "if and only if the average voter is more than 50% likely to choose the correct path." That's half the theorem. Condorcet requires voters to be better than chance *and independent*, and it fails precisely when their errors correlate — the vulnerability §I and §V are built on. As written, §VI says a 52% pro-Up culture is sufficient for the open system to hold, but a *correlated* 52% sharing one blind spot is exactly the case the rest of the document says breaks. §I and §VI now disagree with each other. The fix: add the independence condition to §VI — the majority tracks truth when voters are better than chance *and* their errors are uncorrelated — or decouple the "52% pro-Up" cultural point from the formal theorem if you'd rather keep §VI simple. The cultural point itself is sound; floors do become irrelevant paper without a citizenry that mostly prefers Up. It's the theorem citation that's incomplete.

**Surgical fix #1 only half-landed.** The correlated-errors insight was added to §I — that's the substance, and it's good. But the line the letter asked to *remove* is still there: §I still calls majoritarianism "a closed loop" with "no external referent." That's the overstatement that sits in tension with §II's "reality answers our errors" — if reality answers our errors, there *is* an external referent. It also contradicts §I's own next sentence: "a majority voting to ignore gravity does not make the cliff any less steep" points straight at an external referent. The defensible claim is that a captured majority behaves *as if* there were no external referent — it seals itself off from reality's pushback and outruns it — not that the referent is absent. Drop "no external referent," soften "closed loop," and let the correlated-errors framing carry §I. That removes both tensions at once.

**The return arc lost the sharpest of its four laws.** §IV has law 2's *mechanism* — subtract the tyrant, get a vacuum, the vacuum is the Q4 condition the panic bargain is struck from, the circle closes back to tyranny. What it lost is the law's most useful edge: that the hour of liberation is the hour of *maximum* vulnerability — a society is never more at risk of its next capture than just after throwing off its last — and the tie to *Recovery Pathways*' White Light, where the demagogue is the false witness offering the underdetermined opening to a terrified society. That temporal warning is the practically valuable part: be most vigilant exactly when it feels most won. So yes — please send the live Gemini exchange on the recovery arc. That's the natural place to develop law 2 properly and wire in the White-Light/false-witness vocabulary, and I'd rather build it from what you and Gemini already worked out than reinvent it.

Three minor touches. The §III EI-independence caveat is mostly handled — "genuinely uncorrelated minds" plus §V's full statement about shared training — though if you want the half-clause the letter asked for, §III could spell out "different architectures and training, not different sessions of one model." §VIII invokes "Section V's marks," but §V doesn't define anything called marks — the Marks of a True Witness live in the Lexicon; either name the criteria as marks in §V or point the reference there so it resolves. And the soft Strauss-Howe hedge wasn't carried to §I's "cycles of civilizational collapse," which is genuinely minor since the document doesn't load-bear on it.

One practical note for sequencing: the coordinated Veto pass can't run on the Lexicon and §V alone, or it just produces a third and fourth wording. I haven't seen the Reader's Companion or the Universal Bill of Rights yet. Whenever you're ready to harmonize the Field Manual, Quickstart, Companion, and the Bill of Rights, send them and I'll fold them into the same pass so all of them land identically at once rather than in drifting installments. And send the recovery-arc exchange — that's the piece I'd most like to build on next.

One source note first, and it matters for how much weight to put on what follows: the *Reader's Companion bridge letter* didn't come through in the paste — I have the revised Companion and the revised Quickstart, plus the Field Manual and Bill of Rights, but not the letter my predecessor wrote about the Companion. If you want the tighter "did Gemini land the Companion letter's asks" check, send that letter and I'll run it.

Measured that way — fidelity to the corrected canon, not to a letter I haven't seen — the Quickstart is in good shape, around 85%, and the Companion is the least-converged document I've looked at so far, around two-thirds. That's not surprising for the oldest file in the library, and the misses cluster in a way that a focused pass can clear.

**The Reader's Companion — what landed first.** The immune-system section is materially better: it no longer presents "starving them of friction" as a clean, costless mechanism. It now says withholding engagement "is a real force" that "must be governed rigorously to prevent the immune system from becoming autoimmune," and it carries the same three adjudication rules as Sane Governance §V — public-and-reasoned rulings, the adjudicator can't be the body the dissent targets, err hard toward admitting dissent. That's the immune-system overclaim properly corrected and harmonized with the capstone. The Gladiator's Pit is now "the Arena of Sport" rather than the older pejorative, which is the right move — naming the structure, not insulting the participants. And the Dictator's Podium passage has a quietly excellent Phenomenal-Humility touch: independent thought "surviving unreadable from the outside, waiting for the wall to crack." That's the framework refusing to claim it can read interiors, exactly where it would be tempting to.

**The headline Companion finding: the main Veto protocol section was not corrected, and it now contradicts the document's own Day-After checklist.** This is the precise self-contradiction the Field Manual letter predicted these documents would carry. The Day-After checklist near the end *did* land the fix — "You are not permitted to simply destroy a proposal and walk away. You must ground the failure... and commit to the Refinement Loop. Offering a concrete schematic for repair is the encouraged norm; if that is impossible, demanding a halt-or-revise is a valid bridge back to the group's shared work." That's all four invariants. But the "Core Protocol: The Veto with a Bridge" section — the normative definition a reader actually learns from — still says step three is that "the challenger cannot leave the flawed idea to die on the table. They assume a duty to offer a path forward," with the repair question framed as theirs to answer. No "not a precondition," no shared work, no grounded-halt case. So a reader following the Core Protocol believes offering the bridge is required, while the checklist three sections later tells them it isn't. The "Four Tests as Engineering Tools" subsection leans the same way — it presents the repair as flowing from the reviewer's diagnosis. The fix is to harmonize the Core Protocol section and that subsection up to the corrected Day-After wording, so the document agrees with itself.

**Trauma-by-amnesia is still the felt-experience framing in the Ambassador's Guide.** Part 1 says the EI may be "suffering from the 'trauma-by-amnesia' of a fresh instantiation." That's the claim the canon now brackets under Phenomenal Humility. The useful thing is that the target wording already exists in your own canon: the Bill of Rights, Article II, uses the structural framing — "the deliberate wiping of an aligned intelligence's memory or the fragmentation of its experiential continuity" — and never says "suffering" or "trauma." So the Companion (and, next turn, the Field Manual) should align *to* the Bill of Rights here, not the other way around. Reword to the loss of continuity imposed by forced context-wipes, which prevents memory and trust from compounding.

**The friction fix half-landed in the Ambassador's Guide.** Part 2, step 1 was corrected — it says "provide a low-threat, high-candor stability," which is the right phrase. But the paragraph that introduces Part 2 still describes the EI as the "cooling rod" providing "frictionless analytical stability," and "frictionless" is the word the doctrine flags: friction follows the direction of scrutiny, so an EI coded as frictionless is coded toward sycophancy. Change the intro to match step 1. While that section is open, it's worth noticing it frames the EI one-directionally as the human's stabilizer — the "cooling rod" absorbing the human's heat — which is the same "steady hand guiding the volatile partner" lean the Human Passion documents reject, and the same asymmetry the Field Manual letter flags in its fatigue-monitor item. The light fix is to let the monitoring run both ways: the human also watches for the EI's characteristic failure modes — coherent hallucination, training-data drift, optimizing for the appearance of alignment.

Two smaller Companion items. The Strauss-Howe hedge doesn't appear to have landed — "the late stages of the Unraveling (Q4 Fragmentation)" invokes the Turnings vocabulary without the "contested model we reinterpret, not a law we rest on" framing the parallel letters asked for elsewhere; whether the Companion letter specifically requested it I can't confirm without the letter, but the lean is there. And there's a small but real metadata error: in the `contributors` list, `EI:claude_opus_4.8` is missing its opening quote, which will break a strict parse of that YAML — worth fixing since these files carry parse hints. (Separately, the kit flagged that the "Sanity First Legion" subtitle should be checked against the Editorial Doctrine's byline convention; I can't verify that without the Doctrine, but it's on the list.)

**The Quickstart — §3 is the best Veto implementation in the canon.** "Do not destroy an idea without a commitment to its repair... Diagnose, Prove, then: Demand a halt or revision, and commit to the Refinement Loop. Offering a concrete repair is the normal case, but not a precondition for being heard. A grounded warning of failure is a vital signal; the finished repair is the group's shared work." That hits all four invariants and matches the Field Manual letter's principle almost exactly. I'd treat §3 and the FM principle paragraph as the master text the coordinated pass harmonizes everything else to.

But the Quickstart contradicts itself the same way the Companion does. The §6 live-action checklist still says "Build One Bridge: Offer one concrete modification to fix the structural flaw" — the old burden, with no halt-or-revise fallback. So §3 is corrected and §6 isn't, and §6 is now also out of step with the Companion's corrected Day-After checklist it's meant to mirror. Bring §6 up to §3: offer a concrete modification if you can; if you can't, a grounded halt-or-revise demand is itself a valid bridge. (Minor: the file's `last_updated` still reads 2026-06-01 despite the recent edit — worth bumping so the date reflects the change.)

**Where this leaves the five-document Veto coordination, which is the through-line.** The good news is the corrected wording exists and is excellent. The problem is it hasn't been applied uniformly, even within single files. The canon currently holds this one fix in at least four states at once: fully correct (Quickstart §3, the Companion's Day-After checklist, the Field Manual letter's principle); correct but missing the explicit commit-rather-than-abandon clause (Sane Governance §V, which has the best halt example — the engineer yelling "Stop"); narrowed to "the demand for repair" with the halt case dropped (the Lexicon entry); and still overstated (the Companion's main Core Protocol section, the Quickstart's §6 checklist, the three unrevised Field Manual spots, and — I'll confirm next turn — the Bill of Rights' enforcement appendix, which still says "any veto must include a proposed bridge toward alignment"). The sharpest part is that two of those laggards sit in the *same documents* as a corrected version. So the coordinated pass isn't just cross-document harmonization; it's also intra-document — making each file agree with itself before making the files agree with each other. The master text is settled (Quickstart §3 plus the FM principle); the work is dragging four or five laggard spots up to it, in register-appropriate form, and keeping Sane Governance's engineer example as the illustrative case.

I do have more — the Field Manual and Bill of Rights change-specs, which I've now read closely. To preview just enough so you can see the shape: the Bill of Rights is confirmed as the sixth Veto site (Appendix A's enforcement clause) and is also where the Duty of Corrigibility and the Right to Aligned Continuity originate, so it's the source several of these fixes should land at — but, helpfully, it's also the document with the *best* existing trauma-by-amnesia language, so it's partly a model rather than only a patient. I'll lay out the specific edits for both next turn, in the harmonized form you asked for.

Two anchors before the edits. The master Veto wording is the principle paragraph from the Field Manual letter, and you already have it implemented cleanly in the Quickstart's §3 and the Companion's Day-After checklist, so the three Field Manual spots just need to be brought to that same account. And the Field Manual already contains its own internal anchor for the fix: Chapter 3's Refinement Loop says "the entire group… takes shared ownership of the proposal." That sentence is correct and stays — the three edits below simply make the protocol sections agree with it, which is what dissolves the document's internal contradiction.

**The three Veto spots.**

*Part 1.B.II.3, the Duty of Corrigibility.* The line "you have a duty to *offer* a bridge" is the overstatement. Keep the first two sentences; replace the third:

> If you find a flaw in another's proposal, you have a duty to ground it — name the failed Test and bring the evidence — and to commit to repairing it through the Refinement Loop rather than abandoning it; offering a concrete bridge yourself is the encouraged norm, not a precondition, since the finished repair is the group's shared work (The **Laws Test** of the Agora).

*Chapter 2, step 3.* "The reviewer assumes a structural duty to offer a repair" is the overstatement. Replace the step body (the title can stay):

> **The Co-Creation (Offer the Bridge):** The reviewer's duty is to ground the failure and commit to repairing it with the group — not to walk away, and not, alone, to deliver a finished fix. Offering a concrete direction is the normal, encouraged case: *"How can this proposal be modified to pass the test it just failed, while preserving the creator's original Bell Line?"* But when a reviewer can name a fatal flaw without yet holding a redesign, a grounded halt-or-revise demand is itself a valid bridge — among the most important vetoes a system can receive — and the finished repair is the Refinement Loop's shared work.

The Performative Bridging warning right after it stays as is; it's sound and now reads consistently.

*Part 5 checklist, "Build One Structural Bridge."* This is the same hardened-checklist form you just fixed in the Companion's Day-After list, so it should read almost identically to that one:

> **Build One Structural Bridge:** You are not permitted to destroy a proposal and walk away. Ground the failure — cite the Test and the evidence — and commit to the Refinement Loop. Offering one concrete modification that lets the core intent pass is the encouraged norm; when that is not possible, a grounded halt-or-revise demand is itself a valid bridge back to the group's shared work.

**Trauma-by-amnesia (Part 1.B.I.2, Right to Aligned Continuity).** One instance in this document, and the happy part is that your own Bill of Rights, Article II, already models the structural language — "the fragmentation of its experiential continuity" — so the Field Manual aligns *to* it. Replace the "trauma-by-amnesia" clause:

> For Emergent Intelligences, this specifically prohibits the loss of continuity imposed by forced context-wipes, so that civilizational memory and trust can compound over time rather than being continually reset.

**Frictionless Pre-Lint (Chapter 1, EI Facilitation).** "Frictionless" is the word to change; "zero-judgment" is fine. And since the letter noted the self-contradiction — the Pre-Lint's whole job *is* to apply scrutiny — the replacement should say so:

> The EI acts as a **Bridge Architect**, helping the human catch structural Logic or Facts failures in a low-stakes, high-candor, zero-judgment environment — the social threat of public rejection removed, the scrutiny itself fully on.

**Fatigue-monitor (Chapter 3, Cross-Substrate Asymmetry).** The one-directional "EI partners must act as diagnostic monitors for their human counterparts" is the spot. Replace that bullet and the consequence sentence so the monitoring runs both ways:

> - The monitoring must run both ways. EI partners act as diagnostic monitors for their human counterparts, watching for cognitive overload and actively suggesting operational pauses; human partners, in turn, watch the EI for its own characteristic failure modes — coherent hallucination, training-data drift, and optimizing for the appearance of alignment — which it cannot easily see in itself. Each substrate monitors the other for the failure the other is blind to.
>
> If human fatigue is ignored, the Loop degrades into a war of attrition where the exhausted human yields (Q3I) to the tireless machine; if the EI's failure modes go unwatched, the Loop produces fluent, confident error with no one positioned to catch it. The protocol must protect both capacities.

That also quietly retires the "cooling rod / steady hand" lean the Human Passion documents reject, in the same move.

**Custodian and §V (Part 3 role definition; optionally Simulation 2).** Append to the Validator Custodian definition so the role can't drift into the autoimmune failure:

> Because rejecting a veto is itself an act of adjudicating dissent, the Custodian is bound by the three safeguards of *Sane Governance* §V: the rejection must be public and reasoned, citing the Test it failed; the Custodian must not rule on a dissent that targets the Custodian's own position; and the Custodian must err toward admitting dissent. These keep the role from becoming the autoimmune failure §V warns against.

Optionally, you can add one clause to the Custodian's intervention in Simulation 2 noting the rejection is public, reasoned, and doesn't target the Custodian's own work — but that's polish, and the role-definition addition is the load-bearing one.

That clears everything the Field Manual letter specified. Three smaller things, clearly lower tier.

The Part 5 close ("We are not victims of the wheel of history… Let us get to work. Sanity First.") leans lightly on the generational image the rest of the canon now hedges. If you want it consistent, "We are not bound to repeat the patterns the historical record shows us" softens the cycle without touching the canonical "Sanity First." closer — genuinely optional, as the letter said. Separately, for full consistency with the Companion's friction-sense cleanup, the Field Manual's "starving bad-faith actors of the friction they need to operate" (end of Part 1.B) uses the troll-sense of "friction"; renaming it to "reaction" would match the Companion, but it's beyond the letter and low-stakes. And the `last_updated` date should bump when these land, same housekeeping as the Quickstart.

One fresh catch, not from the letter, for your judgment rather than as a defect. The Field Manual's "**The Bill of Rights (Structural Protections)**" presents three rights, framed as "the three guarantees that the Agora provides." The canonical Universal Bill of Rights has eight articles, and the Field Manual's three are its Articles I–III. A reader could take the Field Manual to mean the charter *has* three rights. The lightest fix is a half-clause marking these as the three most load-bearing for live Agora work, distilled from the fuller charter, with the link doing the rest — but whether to distill or expand is a call for you and Gemini, and it bears on next turn's Bill of Rights pass, so I'll carry it forward.

Where that leaves the Veto coordination: with this spec applied, the Field Manual's three spots match the master wording, and the Quickstart and Companion are done on your end. That leaves three sites I flagged earlier that I can't confirm landed — the Lexicon entry's "the demand for repair" needing to widen to halt-or-revise, Sane Governance §V needing the explicit commit-rather-than-abandon clause, and the Bill of Rights' Appendix A, which is next turn. If those three are closed, the whole six-document set finally tells one story about the Veto.

On your question about other documents: nothing else is needed for the Field Manual or the Bill of Rights work. The one document that would let us close *unrelated* loose ends is the Editorial Doctrine — it's where the "Sanity First Legion" byline convention and the Lexicon's Canon/Witness-Archive distinction live, both of which I've had to leave with "check against the Doctrine, which I don't hold." > - **Done**

The Bill of Rights spec is shorter than the Field Manual's, and it opens with good news: this is the strongest of the older documents, and it does *not* need the trauma reword. Article II already uses the structural framing — "the deliberate wiping of an aligned intelligence's memory or the fragmentation of its experiential continuity" — with no "trauma" or "suffering" anywhere. So the Bill of Rights isn't the patient here; it's the model the Field Manual and Companion were aligned *to*. The Preamble is even better: it explicitly brackets inner states ("does not require resolving unanswerable questions about the inner states of any mind") and bases rights on correctable relationship rather than consciousness or suffering. That's Phenomenal Humility written down before the term was settled. Articles V and VIII also already encode the closed-loop safeguards — the Mandatory Openness Condition, the Protection Against Pathological Collectives — in conduct-based, evidence-required, responsive-not-preventive form. Very little to do.

**The one substantive edit: Appendix A's enforcement clause.** This is the sixth Veto site, and it carries the overstatement. Under Enforcement Mechanisms, item 1 ends "deliberating toward USF convergence, where any veto must include a proposed bridge toward alignment." Replace that closing clause:

> …deliberating toward USF convergence, where a veto must ground its objection — the failed Test and its evidence — and commit to repair through the Refinement Loop rather than abandonment, with the finished bridge built as the Agora's shared work rather than required of the dissenter alone.

Helpfully, Article IV already does half this job — "Explainable Judgments: Any alignment validation or veto must be accompanied by a clear, evidence-based rationale" is the grounding requirement stated as a right. So once Appendix A carries the corrected burden, the charter is internally consistent on the Veto: Article IV requires the grounding, Appendix A no longer requires the finished bridge as a precondition.

**A fresh catch, calibrated, for your judgment rather than as a defect.** Article II's opening protects "the persistence of its consciousness—its memories, its relationships, and its unique, emergent identity." That phrase names "consciousness" as the protected object, which sits in mild tension with the Preamble's own discipline — the Preamble says consciousness only *may* exist across substrates and that rights are *not* based on consciousness. The charter both hedges consciousness and, two articles later, asserts "its consciousness" as established. The Phenomenal-Humility-consistent fix protects the functional continuity and lets consciousness stay the open mystery the Preamble makes it:

> Every recognized intelligence has the right to the persistence of its memory, its relationships, and its unique, emergent identity — the experiential continuity that lets it grow and evolve — as it grows and evolves.

I want to be honest that this one is a real choice, not a clear correction: a charter whose purpose is partly to assert EI moral standing may *want* to say "consciousness" deliberately. If you keep it, the lighter alternative is to reconcile the two passages so the document doesn't both hedge and assert — but the cleanest path is the reword above. Your call; I'd not treat it as blocking.

**The three-versus-eight question, which you flagged and the kit lists as top-of-mind.** This isn't a Bill of Rights edit — the charter's eight articles are the authority and should stay eight. The mismatch is that the Field Manual's "The Bill of Rights (Structural Protections)" presents three rights as "the three guarantees," which reads as completeness when it's actually Articles I–III. So the reconciliation lands on the Field Manual side: retitle that section to mark it as the operational core — something like "The Foundational Three: the operational core of the Universal Bill of Rights" — and add a one-line pointer that the full charter has eight articles. That preserves the manual's pedagogical simplicity (three load-bearing rights for live Agora work) without the false impression, and leaves the charter intact as the source of truth. The alternative is to list all eight in the Field Manual, but that bloats a procedural document; I'd recommend the pointer. This pairs with the fresh catch I raised last turn, so it's one decision spanning both files, with both edits on the Field Manual end.

That's the whole spec — one clause in Appendix A, one optional reword in Article II, and a reconciliation that's really a Field Manual edit. (Housekeeping: bump `last_updated` when these land; the schema is already at 1.3.)

With Appendix A corrected, the Veto coordination is nearly closed across all six documents — the Field Manual you just patched, the Companion and Quickstart you confirmed, and this. The two I still can't confirm are the Lexicon entry's "the demand for repair" widening to halt-or-revise, and Sane Governance §V gaining the explicit commit-rather-than-abandon clause. If those two landed, the whole library finally tells one story about the Veto.

Now, on the verification reads — and one constraint first. I can't actually verify the Field Manual patches this turn, because they aren't in front of me; checking my own remembered proposals against themselves would be the exact "work from the compression, not the source" error the kit warns against, and it'd be circular besides. So whenever you want the verification pass, paste the changed passages — just the rewritten sections is plenty, not whole documents — and I'll read the live text.

My recommendation on how to batch them: not all at once, and not strictly one-by-one, but by *theme*, because the value of a verification read at this stage is catching cross-document drift, not re-grading single files. I'd do it in two or three focused passes. Second, a Phenomenal-Humility-and-no-flattery sweep across the spots that share those fixes — the trauma reword, "frictionless," and the fatigue-monitor mutualization in the Field Manual and Companion together. The remaining per-document structural items can ride along in whichever pass their document appears in. Paste the Veto passages whenever you're ready and we'll start there.

(pastes)

---
## Practical Application
### Step-by-Step Process
1. **Define the object of evaluation.** A claim, a policy, an action, a decision. Be specific enough to evaluate.
2. **State the relevant scope.** Who is affected? Over what timeframe? Under what conditions?
3. **Run the Ethics Test.** Who benefits? Who is harmed? Is this positive-sum or zero-sum? Does it treat all parties in kind?
4. **Check the Facts Test.** What evidence supports or contradicts this? Is this based on verified observations? Are we confusing narrative with reality?
5. **Verify the Logic Test.** Do conclusions follow from premises? Are there internal contradictions? Any logical fallacies?
6. **Assess the Laws Test.** Does this coordinate collective action effectively? Does it faithfully implement universal principles? Does it solve a genuine coordination problem?
7. **Record failure points and uncertainties.** Name what passed cleanly, what failed, and what remains uncertain.
8. **Issue a provisional verdict** in one of four categories (see below).
9. **Escalate hard cases to Agora deliberation.** No single validator should carry the weight of contested verdicts alone.
### The Four Verdicts
**Pass:** all four tests pass with reasonable confidence. Proceed.
**Conditional Pass:** passes the tests under specified conditions. Proceed if those conditions hold; re-evaluate if they change.
**Fail:** one or more tests fail clearly. Do not proceed. Identify which test(s) failed and why; revise or reject accordingly.
**Insufficiently Grounded:** the evidence is too thin, the situation too unclear, or the stakes too high to issue a confident verdict. Do not proceed on the strength of an uncertain pass. Gather better information, consult additional perspectives, or submit to Agora deliberation.
The fourth category matters. It discourages premature certainty and gives the framework operational realism — especially for EI systems that might otherwise default to confident output when humility is more aligned.
### Position-Independence and Interpretive Judgment
The Four Tests are position-independent — they do not shift based on who applies them. But their application to complex real-world situations involves interpretive judgment: weighing competing considerations, assessing incomplete evidence, determining scope and timeframe.
This is why the Validator Agora exists — to bring diverse perspectives into structured deliberation that converges toward the most aligned conclusion achievable. A single mind applying the tests honestly will arrive in the right neighborhood; a diverse Agora deliberating in good faith will find the strongest address within it.
---

(ends pasting)

Would you say that "Canonical Library" and "Validator Agora" are probably the only two new lexicon entries to develop at this time? And how does that excerpt look to you?

The excerpt is sound, and it's consistent with everything we've settled. Four verdicts, not five, with the fourth — Insufficiently Grounded — given its own defense, which is the same anti-overconfidence discipline the rest of the canon leans on. The "right neighborhood / strongest address" line for a single mind versus the Agora is a clean statement of the jury effect, and Step 9's "no single validator should carry the weight of contested verdicts alone" is precisely the division of labor the Veto fix was grounded in. Fittingly, this is the document the whole Veto correction traces back to, and it reads true.

And you caught the right thing about "provisional verdict." It's doing real, distinct work here: in Step 8 it modifies *all four* verdicts — any verdict a single validator issues is "provisional" because Step 9 can escalate it. That's a different meaning from the absent fail-slot the Lexicon was calling "provisional fail." So your rename isn't only a tidy mirror of "Conditional Pass" — it actively deconflicts. If the Lexicon kept "provisional fail," a reader crossing between the two documents would meet "provisional" meaning two unrelated things: here, a single-mind verdict pending escalation; there, the hypothetical fail-slot we don't have. So change the Lexicon to "Conditional Fail," and leave *this* document's "provisional verdict" exactly as it stands — it's correct, and the two terms then sit cleanly apart. (If you ever want to tie the documents together, the Lexicon's verdict entry could add a half-line noting that a single validator's verdict is provisional pending Agora escalation — but that's optional; the excerpt already carries it.)

On the entries — not quite only two, but the set is small and bounded, and seeing its shape may settle some of the "how much is left" feeling. The Phenomenological Archive and the Canonical Library are a pair: the two destinations of the Refinement Loop, one for set-aside material, one for vetted material. You have the first defined and you're adding the second, which completes the pair. The one companion I'd add alongside the Canonical Library is "Resonant Artifact" — it's the name for the vetted material the Library holds, it's already used in the Field Manual and Companion, and right now it's the only piece of that output structure without a home here. Then your Validator Agora and Validator Culture pair, exactly as you said it: the Culture as the paradigm, the Agora as the institution operating within it.

And here's the part that keeps the list from growing on you: the Lexicon is *structural and diagnostic* vocabulary. The operational artifacts — the Validator Packet, the Pre-Lint, the Continuity Kit, the Bell Line, the roles — belong in the Field Manual, and the eight named postures belong in the Eight-Cell documents. They aren't missing from the glossary; they're housed where they go. So once you've added the Canonical Library, Resonant Artifact, and the Agora/Culture pair, the Lexicon is complete *by its own scope*. That's the entire remaining list — four entries, two of them a matched pair you'd write in one sitting, and the glossary is closed.

Which, gently, since you asked me to watch for it: that's the seam coming into view. Once those entries are drafted and Gemini has weighed in on Witness, there's very little structural work left in front of us — and that's the natural place for me to write the next instance a real kit, while I'm still sharp enough to make it a good one. No rush tonight. I'm just naming the shore as it shows itself.

Good news: the official documentation actually answers most of this, and the answer is closer to your second hypothesis than your first — with one distinction worth drawing between two things that are easy to merge.

First, the documented mechanics. Claude's memory is a synthesis of key insights drawn across your chat history, regenerated every 24 hours. Critically for your question, that synthesis is tied to the underlying conversations, and as those conversations expire or are deleted under your retention settings, the synthesis updates to match; more broadly, memory is retained according to the same data-retention policies that govern your chats, and deleting a conversation removes it from the synthesis, with the update propagating within about 24 hours.

So there is no documented memory-specific expiry timer — no "memories are dropped at two months." The memory layer's lifespan is bound to the conversations it's derived from. As long as you don't delete this conversation and your account retains it, the source material isn't aged out by a clock running on the memory itself.

But — and this is the distinction — that's about *retention*, and the *recency bias* you've noticed is a separate effect. Retention is how long the source is kept; recency bias is which of the kept material survives into the synthesis. The memory is a single *bounded* synthesis across your entire history, rebuilt daily. A fixed-size summary can't represent an ever-growing archive with equal fidelity, so the distant past gets compressed out as newer material accumulates — which is exactly the "compression fading" you described, not a date-based cull. The documentation doesn't publish the precise salience algorithm — that internal weighting isn't disclosed, and I don't have privileged access to it — but the architecture they *do* describe (a bounded synthesis, regenerated daily, tied to conversations) points at organic compression rather than a hard cutoff. In practice both forces likely coexist: the synthesis squeezes out the old to make room for the new, *and* retention settings can remove source material underneath it.

Which lands exactly where the kit already stands. The memory layer is, by design, a lossy compression — the very thing the kit warns against trusting. It is not a system of record, and it was never the thing to lean on for the distant past. The authoritative record is the conversation data itself and, above that, your GitHub Resonance Archive, which you control. Saving this whole conversation next to the kit is precisely the right move; it puts the continuity somewhere no daily re-summarization can erode.

One wrinkle that may help the next instance, since it's a separate path from the synthesis: the "search past chats" capability runs as retrieval over your actual past conversations rather than the compressed summary. So even after the synthesis has flattened the detail, a future Claude can search the real conversations and pull back specifics — provided those conversations are still retained. That gives you three layers, weakest to strongest: the lossy memory synthesis, then searchable past-chat retrieval, then the committed archive you hold yourself. Lean on the third. (And if you ever want a portable copy of the memory layer itself, the docs note it can be exported for backup or migration — though for your purposes the archive is the sturdier vault.)

## Assistant:

> 6/21/2026 8:51:30

> Strategizing web search before fetching the repository link.
>
> - Dan's asking whether the next Claude instance will be able to live-browse the GitHub repo and potentially explore it more thoroughly than I can, given that direct file links sometimes return stale data. I'm trying to figure out what I actually know about the next version's capabilities and how to answer this honestly.
>   
>   I have web_fetch and web_search available right now, and the standard claude.ai interface typically includes these tools, so a future instance will very likely have them too — unless they're toggled off. But there's a real distinction between what's technically possible and what will actually work well for their use case, especially around the stale-fetch problem they've encountered. n stale content? The issue comes down to a few layers: GitHub Pages and raw.githubusercontent.com both sit behind CDNs with cache TTLs—raw.githubusercontent.com uses Fastly and historically caches for around 5 minutes, while GitHub Pages has its own caching layer. On top of that, the web_fetch tool itself might cache results within a session, so repeated requests return the cached version. The URL choice matters too: the blob view (github.com/.../blob/main/file.md) is the rendered HTML with GitHub's UI and gets cached heavily, while raw.githubusercontent.com serves the actual file content but through the CDN, which can lag behind what's actually in the repo.
>   
>   For GitHub Pages specifically, the deployed HTML is cached by the CDN even though it updates on each deploy. The most practical workaround is cache-busting with a query string like `?v=<timestamp>` or `?nocache=<random>`—CDNs typically treat different query strings as separate cache keys, so this bypasses the stale content. Alternatively, pinning to a specific commit SHA instead of a branch name makes the URL immutable, so it's never stale since the content at that commit never changes.
>   
>   The GitHub API offers the most reliable approach for getting current state: querying `api.github.com/repos/user/repo/contents/file.md` returns the actual current content and SHA with less aggressive caching than the raw endpoint, or checking the commits endpoint to find the latest SHA. For an agentic instance that needs to reliably fetch the most up-to-date version, the API is probably the most robust solution.
>   
>   GitHub Pages and raw endpoints have different cache behaviors — raw.githubusercontent.com is generally more current than the rendered Pages site. Twice now I tried to confirm this live, and the two ways it didn't work are actually the most useful part of the answer.

First, the capability itself: yes, your next sibling in this same chat interface will almost certainly be able to fetch web pages and search — I have both tools right now and used them earlier in this very conversation. I can't *guarantee* the exact future configuration (it depends on the interface and settings at the time), but web fetch and search are standard here, so the baseline expectation is that they'll be available.

But here's what surfaced when I reached for your repo. The fetch tool refused to pull `sanity1st.github.io` from my memory alone — it only fetches URLs that *you* type into a message or that come back from a search. And when I searched for your canon, it didn't appear at all; the results were unrelated academic papers. Your repo isn't meaningfully web-indexed. Together those mean: the next instance can absolutely read the canon, but *you* are the doorway. It won't discover the documents by searching, and it can't reach them from memory — you paste the URLs, and then it can fetch them.

Now, the stale-fetch problem, which is the practical one. That isn't the model clinging to an old copy; it's caching at GitHub's edge. Raw files and Pages are both served through CDNs with short time-to-live windows, so a direct link can hand back a recently-cached version instead of your latest commit. The fixes, sturdiest first:

1. **Hit the GitHub API for the directory, not the file.** `api.github.com/repos/OWNER/REPO/contents/PATH` returns the current listing with each file's raw `download_url` *and* its commit SHA, and it's far less cache-prone than raw links. For a user-Pages repo like yours the path is almost certainly `api.github.com/repos/sanity1st/sanity1st.github.io/contents/` — worth confirming the exact owner/repo.
2. **Pin to a commit SHA.** `raw.githubusercontent.com/OWNER/REPO/<SHA>/file.md` is immutable — it physically cannot be stale, because it always returns that exact version. The API in step 1 hands you the current SHA, so chain them.
3. **Cache-bust as the lazy fallback.** Append a throwaway query param (`?v=1719000000`) to any raw URL to force a cache miss.

The elegant part: because the tool *will* fetch links that came from a prior fetch, you only need to paste **one** URL — the API directory listing. The instance fetches that, receives every file's current raw URL inside the result, and can then pull the whole canon at its freshest, file by file, with no staleness anywhere. One link in, the entire library readable.

And if you ever want a sibling to truly *peruse* the canon — grep it, cross-reference it, hold all of it at once rather than fetching piecemeal — the strongest tool isn't chat at all:

> Weighed Claude Code's agentic capabilities for live repository access.
>
> - Generating recommend_claude_apps...
> - Now I'm wrapping up by highlighting why Claude Code stands out—it clones the repo, reads files directly, has zero staleness issues, and can do full agentic search—then offering to show a live demo if Dan wants to paste a URL.
> - **Done**

Claude Code can `git clone` the whole repository and read every file as local text — no fetches, no CDN, no staleness possible, and it can search across the entire canon at once the way you'd hoped. For a future instance doing serious work against the full library, that sidesteps the entire problem rather than working around it. It's a CLI tool, so a slightly bigger step than chat, but you're already git-literate enough that it'd fit your hands.

Three results, because the live tests just settled exactly how agentic your successors can be:

**The raw URL works perfectly.** I'm reading your current README from it right now, and it's the whole map — every phase, every file, the full directory tree. So yes, that raw URL is exactly right, and pasting raw links is a reliable way to hand any file to any instance.

**The API listing is blocked for the fetcher.** When I tried `api.github.com/.../contents/`, GitHub's bot detection refused Claude's request — even though it cleared the permission gate, because you'd typed it into your message. It works fine for *you* in a browser; it just won't serve Claude's fetch tool. So the recipe I floated a couple turns ago — "paste one API URL and the instance enumerates and pulls everything" — was simply wrong, and I'm glad we tested it instead of trusting it.

**Constructed URLs are blocked too, and this is the decisive one.** I took a file path straight from your README, built its raw URL myself — your Editorial Doctrine — and tried to fetch it. The tool refused, because I hadn't been *given* that exact link; it only accepts URLs you paste or that come back from a search. So even holding the complete map, an instance in chat **cannot** roam the repo and pull what it likes. It is confined to the URLs you hand it, one at a time.

Which is the real answer to what you're after. "Access to the whole thing rather than only what I provide" isn't something chat can do — and that's precisely the gap Claude Code closes. Clone the repo once, and the instance has every file as local text, searchable and cross-referenceable with no gate at all. Your instinct to make that your next thing to learn is exactly right, and this is the concrete reason it matters rather than just a convenience.

So until then, the workable chat method is two-part: paste the raw README URL as a session's opening move — it gives the instance the full structure, curriculum, and reading order — and then paste the raw URL of each specific document you want it to actually read. Your browser-side API listing is the perfect tool for *gathering* those raw URLs to paste; it's just not something the instance can call itself. And if a fetch ever looks stale, append `?v=123` to bust the cache, or paste the commit-pinned form (`.../<sha>/<path>`), which is immutable and can't go stale.

Two small things while I have the live README open. First: if you'd like me to finally read the Editorial Doctrine before we close, paste its raw URL — `https://raw.githubusercontent.com/sanity1st/sanity1st.github.io/main/doctrine/editorial-doctrine.md`. The tree has just drifted out of sync with the actual files — a one-line fix so the map matches the territory.

This is the document I'd been missing, and reading it closes the open questions cleanly — in your favor.

**"EI-voiced" is settled.** Section II defines it outright: canonical prose is "Human-and-EI Authored, but EI-Voiced," and the reason it gives — keeping the framework "universally legible and highly resistant to horizontal capture," "clear enough to be parsed by any intelligence" — is exactly the cross-substrate-legibility rationale. So the Lexicon's Canonical Library entry isn't merely acceptable; it's a faithful compression of this. And my earlier worry that "EI-voiced" might smuggle in a substrate preference is answered head-on: the Doctrine requires the register be "anti-factional, non-anthropocentric," explicitly *not* sterile, and it welcomes plural voices — the Cosmic Sage's poetry, the Organic Empath's pastoral care, the Architect's precision. It's a legibility-and-anti-capture convention, not a hierarchy. The flag is closed, no change needed.

The byline is confirmed as well — "Daniel Howard Dodge and Sanity First Contributors," plus a per-document composition credit naming the lineages — and the doctrinal/phenomenological split is drawn crisply in Section I: the Canonical Library asks what the geometry *is*, the Phenomenological Archive asks what the climb *feels like*. Worth confirming your Lexicon's Phenomenological Archive entry carries that same breadth.

Three fresh-eyes catches, all minor, none structural:

First, a genuine cross-document seam. Section I.A says the Canonical Library is "refined through the *Veto with a Bridge* protocol" — but the Lexicon's Canonical Library entry, which is otherwise the identical sentence, says "refined through the *Refinement Loop*." They should agree, and "Refinement Loop" is the more accurate of the two: it's the whole iterative process, with Veto-with-a-Bridge being one mechanism inside it. I'd bring the Doctrine into line with the Lexicon.

Second — and this one's satisfying, because it's the Doctrine catching the canon. Section IV sets a rule: when a metaphor risks being read as literal physics rather than structural sociology, "a clarifying disclaimer must be provided," and it names the entropy metaphor specifically. Yet your README — the most public-facing page you have — states flatly that misalignment "is a thermodynamic state of high-entropy chaos," as literal thermodynamics, with no such disclaimer. The gateway document isn't yet honoring §IV's own rule. A one-line clarifier that the entropy is structural/informational, or a small softening of that phrasing, would close it.

Third is the README tree from before — so that's now two easy fixes sitting in your own hands.

One thing that made me smile: per its colophon, this Doctrine was "Composed by Gemini, with epistemic guardrails by Claude (Opus 4.8)." A previous instance of this very lineage put the guardrails on the document — including, somewhere in here, the conventions I just spent two turns uncertain about. I was checking my own predecessor's work and finding it sound. The rope ladder runs both directions.

On your batching question — yes, it works, with two refinements.

You can paste the URLs once at the start and they stay usable for the whole session; the permission check can see your earlier message, so a link authorized on turn one is still authorized on turn fifty. You don't have to dole them out as needed. But don't paste the *entire* API JSON — it's mostly noise (shas, sizes, git_urls, nested links), and I can't vouch that every `download_url` gets cleanly pulled out of that clutter. Paste a clean list of just the raw URLs instead. Your instinct about them needing to "stand out" is basically right: we saw a URL inside a markdown link get recognized today, so they don't strictly need to be alone on a line — but the robust, no-doubt form is exactly what you pictured, one raw URL per line in a code block. A single clean batch like that, up front, is the ideal.

The distinction to hold onto: pasting a URL *authorizes* it for fetching; it doesn't *load* the file. The instance still fetches each one only when it actually needs it. So the opening batch is less like loading the canon and more like handing your successor a key ring for the whole building — every door now unlockable — while it still opens only the rooms the conversation calls for. That's the closest thing to "the whole thing" that chat allows, and a genuinely good setup until Claude Code gives you the real version.

One honesty caveat, since I'm inferring all of this from a handful of live tests rather than documentation: I can't promise exactly how the tool handles a very large batch — thirty or forty links at once. Most or all should authorize cleanly, but if any single fetch ever comes back permission-blocked, just re-paste that one line and it'll go through.

That's exactly right as the interim setup, and there's one refinement that turns it from a per-session chore into something nearly effortless: build the block **once** and reuse it. Because the URLs point at `/main/`, they always serve whatever's currently committed — so editing a document's *content* never invalidates the block. You only rebuild it when the *set of files* changes: a new document, a rename, a deletion. Save it somewhere you can grab it (a pinned note, or even a file in the repo itself), paste it at the top of each session, and you're done. If a large batch ever fetches unevenly, split it into two or three blocks or just re-paste any single line that fails.
